=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A very nice, fun, light game.  Players take turns placing candy camels on the board, scoring points.  Game ends when all camels of one color are gone.  Extra points are awarded for placing the most camels of a color.  There's clearly strategy involved, but gameplay isn't that deep or complex.  During/after my second play, it started looking like a min/maxing kind of game.  Light, easy and fun, but not deep enough to really hold me.  Good medium-length filler.  Also a good introductory game.  We call them candy camels because of the pastel colors (they are NOT edible).  I recommend trying it - it really is excellent filler for (at least) occasional play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1st play: This may be my new favorite Knizia game.  Right up there with Ra mechanics-wise and more appealing to look at.  Area control Go-style game that's much faster, easier to jump into, and supports up to 5.  Balancing between controlling areas of the board, touching Oasis, and stealing water tiles is fun and creates difficult decisions.  My only real complaint is that the green and blue camels are a bit too similar in color, and I usually have no problems with color.

2nd play: All the paths seem roughly equal in strength, there's a lot of opportunity to block and outplay others, with tension about which objective to pursue.  It feels similar to Go, but plays 4 and with the palm trees and bonus tiles, helps simplify where to place your attention.

RATING: 6.5
As long as you dont play it all the time, its a pretty nice light tactics game. A lot of variety because of the different ways to score points and camel colours. No difficulties regarding the distinguishability of the colours. Extra point for beautiful cover+board+material. May be an abstract but doesnt feel like one. My first Knizia game, I wonder if the others are as likeable as this. 3 or 4 players recommended.

The importance of the "place your camels" round at the beginning is crucial. If you let others get huge territories or many oasis points without conflict, you better have that advantage as well. After about 10 plays it got a little "samey" but because of the beautiful cover and box of the german version I'll keep it.

The game play is deep for it's playing time, but even better the problems are spacial and luck-free, which is a good contrast from what most other games offer.

Really, it's very much like an abstract in this sense (which isn't my favorite genre), but it doesn't feel like a true abstract.  The theme works pretty well, and, at least with 3 players, there's just enough uncertainty about what will happen before it's your turn again that you can calculate but not too deeply.

RATING: 6.5
It's a nice game, with rules incredibly simple for such a deep game. The components are top-notch, and they even entice non-gamers to give it a try.

Which is not recommended, by the way. This can be a really mean game, and quite unforgiving for newcomers.

And here relies the only downfall of the game. While it meets most qualities to a great game (plays quick, easy to learn, nice bits), the actual learning process is very, very steep.

I like to play it, but it's really difficult to play it with people at my own level... if introduced to newbies, I'll trounce them. If I play with experienced players at Ludagora, they'll trounce me.

UPDATE: Still a nice game, but no longer one I'd usually want to play. Down to a 6.5.

RATING: 6

You also need to take the points you are in most danger of losing first, while leaving safe plays for last. Lots of depth to this little game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Deutscher Spiele Preis 4th Place 1998[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 10/16/05, Rated: 7[/i]

It's a cloudy, drizzly day today, so I thought I would introduce my family to another recent acquisition.  The sunny colors of the game board along with the five flavors of candy pastel camels provided a great contrast to the dreary skies outside.

Three games are behind us now, played with four people, then three, then two.  [i]Through the Desert[/i] is a fun little game.  The most lucrative scoring mechanism is to enclose a portion of the board with your own camels, that are also of the same color.  This mechanism feels a bit like [i][GAMEID=188].[/i]  There are ancillary scoring opportunities such as capturing "water holes," linking to oases, and having the longest caravan of a particular color.  In a tightly-contested game, these other scoring options can make or break you.

Initial placement of your "caravan leaders" is absolutely vital to winning  the game.  I cannot emphasize this point enough.  It also means that a player with poor leader placement is probably doomed from the outset, even if she doesn't know it yet.  This dynamic is so severe that it limits the fun people have while playing if they realize their own predicament.

[i]Through the Desert[/i] is a lot of fun, and the family is already asking for more.  It definitely earns a solid score of "7."  Maybe its score will creep up when we all become more proficient at it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Easy to learn and quick to play, yet strategic and confrontational, with lots of tension and turn angst.  This game is reduced down to its most basic elements, but this is precisely why it is so wonderful. The fact that a turn consisting only of 'Place two camels on the board' can be so difficult is absolutely wonderful. The only real fault I can find with it is that at times it seems too predictable -- you play there, so I have to play here, etc., but then again it's also a part of the game's appeal: players can actually make threats and force opponents to respond (unlike some other Eurogames where it feels like one player's choice doesn't truly impact the next player's decisonmaking process). The multiple sources of victory points provide strategic interest and depth in a game that would otherwise be too cut and dry. The game does lack the ability to make truly 'surprising' and profound moves (as in Chess, [perhaps to a lesser extent] Tigris & Euphrates, wargames, etc.), but on the other hand, the fact that Through the Desert is so straightforward while providing such a rich strategic experience is a large part of the reason it is such a great game. Through the Desert is a great 'gateway' game (due to the simple turn structure and transparent scoring mechanisms) that has enough depth to be played for years. It is not praised nearly as often as it should be. (2009-03-21; revised 2014-02)

[url=http://www.rpg.net/reviews/archive/11/11919.phtml]This review[/url] provides some very insightful comments as well: [i]TtD's biggest strength is that it's a game of brinkmanship. Every turn is agonizing because there are multiple actions that you want to take, and you have to constantly worry about other players taking the same opportunities that you're hoping to take advantage of. ... [Due to the multiple sources of points, there is not a single] good answer for winning every game. There are also lots of tradeoffs, as you give up one victory option to pursue another. ... The 8 oasis spaces and (to a lesser extent) the variable water holes can really change the game around a lot.[/i]

RATING: N/A
I got rid of this, because there were elements where a person who had played just 2-3 games of this, would be performing so much better than a person new to the game...it would be an absolute bloodbath.  The mechanic of not having the same colored camels touch each other, and all the different ways of scoring...it just...  didn’t make me feel good about introducing it to people.  "Oh, sorry, didn't you remember that small deserts were worth 2 and large worth 3...and then claiming the most camels was worth 10.  Oh, and they can't touch if they're the same color.  Sorry you forgot about that during set-up."  

It's ok to teach a game, but you have to be able to correct people AS they're making the mistake, and I hate correcting them like 7 moves down the line when they thought they could do something and couldn't...and this game fosters that situation.

Fantastic on the tablets though.

So, playing it: 8
If I have to explain the rules: 5

-----------------

I thought about this a bit.  Way too much points-soup.  Which, on its own as a game mechanic it isn't bad...  but pretending you can keep all the different dimensions of scoring together in your head as you judge your opponents intentions, motivations, and scoring...HA!

While I know that's the point, to be surprised by something you weren't thinking about and to catch your opponent by something they weren't thinking about...  you really can't pretend to think you know what's going on in the game even when you do fully understand and grok all the different ways to score points.

So I don't really like that in a game unless I'm always playing with people that know it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A typical 5C game; Chaining candy colored camel caravans. On a hex board.

In your turn you take two camels of one of the five colors and add them to one of your five caravans on the board. Equal colors of others' caravans can't touch and you gain points by collecting oase tiles, reaching palm trees, enclosing areas and having the longest caravan in each color.

Simple rules, but enough to ponder. Since the board is pretty tight there's multiple fronts where you're being contested by other players. Each turn it's constantly prioritizing the best tactical move. There is strategy in guessing your opponents plans and outmaneuvering them.

It's a fun filler that stays challenging enough over time. You can, however, be seriously handicapped by opponents at the beginning of the game when claiming starting positions. It's hard to overcome a crowded starting place. Also, it's not that friendly for the color-impaired among us. In lamp light I'm struggling a bit to distinguish some of the pastel pieces.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I think this game is a winner. First off the rules are very simple but there is a strategy in the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Yet a great game from Knizia. Sure, it's abstract, but I'm not a sucker for themes at all, and I adapt to all of the pasted on themes, so I feel like I'm making caravans in the desert...

I really like the open-information board - every player has access to the same information, so there is no luck and the winner is probably the player who played best, and I like those games. The only thing you do on your turn is placing 2 camels, so the gameplay itself is extremely easy, but it's actually much harder than it sounds as there are lots of places you want to put them, so you need to think where they will benefit you the most. So, the fact that you want to do more thing on your turn than you are allowed to, along with the various ways you can score points, makes this game a game I like quite a lot!

It's also one of the few games my wife actually requests to play, so that's a huge bonus! Knizia really is the master of board game designs, and he has designed a lot of my favorite games (not those in my personal top 3, but definitely a couple in my top 20 and one in my top 5 (Ra)).

RATING: 8
Rated "8" after one play with three players. Wow. Surprisingly cutthroat. But it plays much lighter than what is essentially a no-luck game. Each turn, all you do is place two camels. But the agonizing choice is that you've got 6 things you want to do... and only two camels.

I expect the two player game is a bit more forgiving, and this has potential to be a solid "spouse game."

Update: Finally played this with two players. It's strange to have one caravan color of your own that your opponent does not. The board is more forgiving with two, and it seems more about enclosing large areas. I think my favorite player count is three players. If my wife could get past the pastel colors, we'd be playing this with more regularity.

RATING: 8.5
Quite possibly best with three and weakest with five.  Two player only seems to work well when both players are clearly balanced in skill.  Delightfully tense and deep in attempting to deduce and predict what trade-offs the other players will make.  Understanding the incentive mesh can be surprisingly difficult.  

The metagame of manipulating suggestive opportunism is particularly delicious.  In fact the larger the player count the more the game seems to involve creating "you and him fight while I win" situations.  The problem is that with larger player counts the control of those incentive fights is so ambiguous as to be almost moot.

RATING: 5
A light-medium weight tile (camel) laying game suitable for both the hardcore and casual. Scoring in this game comes fast and furious, and initial placements seem to be very, very important in dictating what and where the important battles are going to be. Only difficult thing to keep track of in this title is the gamut of scoring opportunities, I would almost say too many in that one can inadvertantly score points for having long chains, racking up the oasi bonuses, and picking up watering holes along the way with the enclosure mechanism being very potentially powerful but generally hard to enact against wary foes. 

I like the title, but I think much of it has to do with the presentation and speed relative to its ancestor. Without pastel camels and plastic oasis trees this game would likely lose major appeal points for me, as it stands, willing to play most of the time, but mainly because it's fast and I have no real urge to own it, and would certainly never request it. Was dying to get back to it after my first two plays, cooler after the third, after the fourth I was still willing to play it, but wouldn't be suggesting it, after the fifth I am starting to wonder if I have much of a response to this at all anymore. The experience of playing this is far from unenjoyable, it is just a little too basic for my tastes, and though I do like the control vis a vis Samurai's random tile draw I still don't find myself enthused about this in any way beyond the presentation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I cannot remember why I decided to buy this game: I think it was because of the pictures here on the Geek. I bought this game without trying it first, and have not regretted it one bit. Just place two camels each round, and work your way from there: do you go for territories? Long caravans? Oasis control? Your choice. With two players this game is excruciatingly tense and quick. With four players it is different, and slightly slower, but still highly enjoyable. At the start the board is so cluttered that one quickly decides to ignore one or even two of their caravans and concentrate on the remaining ones instead, which is shame, sort-of. The game clearly belongs in Knizia's top 3, though not at first place because of the clunkiness of the set-up and odd (but understandable) asymmetry when actually commencing play. Nevertheless, once the first camels are on the board, the game refuses to let you go, drawing you deeper and deeper into its heart devoid of unnecessary frills. This is an abstract game in a delightful setting with an extremely high what I'd call in Dutch 'aaibaarheidsfactor'&mdash;'pettability factor'&mdash;which always meets with approval whenever it hits the table.

Upon (many!) repeated plays, I found that this game wears well with age, and after giving Go a go, I've come to appreciate it even more. It isn't exactly Go, of course, but there are elements which remind me of it. The fact that you encircle other caravans, for example, while trying to deny them space so they can't create territory, for example. The fact that making a move here automatically weakens you there. There are differences too: Go doesn't know about important spots on the board as does Through the Desert, so the game play focuses on other elements too besides making territory: making long caravans, and connecting them to oases and water holes. It's interesting to speculate on these aspects.

RATING: 8.25
Spatial games aren't always my thing, but for some reason this one just connected with the right parts of my brain and it was love at first sight.

Players spread their groups of camels in various lovely pastel colors around the board, trying to pick up water tiles, connect to oases, or enclose an area with just one color.  Skillful positioning and blocking is key, and there is a lot to keep an eye on.

I loved that there were several ways to improve your score, so even as you got cut off in one area you knew there would be other opportunities.  This is a solid winner, and I'm only sorry it took me so long to play it!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Components: Easy-to-handle camels, scoring tokens, and scoring tiles. The infamous colors are unusual, but playable.

Experience: I enjoy the four different ways to score, the simplicity of my turn, and the quick pacing of the game (its marriage of slow, always-have-a-turn's-warning pacing with not-enough-time-to-do-everything-I-want anxiety). The more I play it the more convinced I am of its brilliance.

Beginning with the initial placements, the game is immediately tense. Every move is a battle to solidify boundaries, to fork opponents and force tough choices, and to position myself for maximum flexibility. It's interesting to gauge the relative worth of a scoring opportunity, weighed against the effort to achieve it. And I must always keep a close watch on the camel supply.

First Played: 2004-06-20
Last Played: 2014-11-21
Plays (15):
2 players: 6
3 players: 2
4 players: 4
5 players: 3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Date: 2001

Vous incarnez une des cinq tribus de bédouins qui développent leurs caravanes dans le désert. A vous d'occuper au mieux les lieux stratégiques les plus lucratifs.

But du jeu : avoir le plus de points en fin de partie

Préparation
A 4 ou 5 joueurs tout le plateau est utilisé, à 2 et 3 joueurs seule la grande partie est utilisée ( un trait foncé sépare le plateau). Le plateau est divisé en hexagones, et chaque hexagone ne peut contenir qu'une pièce, chameau ou palmier.
Dans ce jeu les joueurs ne sont pas identifiés par une couleur de chameau mais par la couleur de cavaliers. Au début de la partie les joueurs reçoivent 6 cavaliers de couleurs identiques plus un chameau gris et un chameau dans chacune des cinq couleurs. Le joueur placera un de ses cavaliers sur le chameau gris et le posera devant lui,celui ci servant simplement à identifier la couleur du joueur. Un cavalier ira sur chacun des chameaux de couleur. A 5 joueurs, chacun se défausse d’un chameau de couleur différente avec son cavalier.
Les autres chameaux sont triés par couleur et placés à côté du plateau pour former les 5 réserves communes .
A 2 joueurs, on retire 10 chameaux par couleur.
A 3 joueurs, on retire 5 chameaux par couleur.
Les cinq palmiers sont placés sur cinq des cases illustrées avec un palmier, il représentent les oasis. Les points d’eau sont mélangés et placés face visible sur chaque case marquée d’un cercle ainsi que sur les cases palmier inoccupées. Le matériel non utilisé est mis de coté.
Le jeu commence par une phase de position : chaque joueur pose l’un après l’autre un de ses chameau sur un hexagone libre, qui ne soit pas adjacent à un oasis ou à un autre cavalier et qui ne contient pas de point d’eau. Au premier tour les joueurs s’efforceront de jouer une couleur de chameau qui n’a pas encore été jouée.
Quand les 5 tours de positionnement ( 4 à 5 joueurs ) est terminé le jeu commence.

Le jeu
A son tour, le joueur choisit deux chameaux de la réserve ( identiques ou non) et les place sur des hexagones libres pour agrandir ses propres caravanes. Il faut placer un chameau de manière à ce qu’il touche un autre chameau de la même couleur et appartenant joueur. Il est donc possible d’agrandir sa caravane dans tous les sens et d’avoirs des embranchements secondaires.
Mais il est interdit de poser un chameau qui relierait deux caravanes de même couleur, et donc deux joueurs. Ainsi tout est clair et on sait exactement quel est le propriétaire de tel ou tel chameau.

Il y quatre façons de gagner des points :

1- Occuper les points d’eau : Le premier joueur à poser un chameau sur un point d’eau, récupère le jeton et gagne autant de points que sa valeur ( 1, 2 ou 3 ). Les jetons sont placés devant soi.

2- Relier les oasis : Lorsqu’une caravane arrive sur un des hexagones qui entourent une oasis, le joueur gagne un pion oasis qui vaut 5 points. Attention une caravane ne peut gagner de point qu’une seule fois par oasis, si d’autres chameaux sont posés par la suite autour de l’oasis il n’y aura pas de gain de points. Par contre le joueur pourra gagner des points en amenant une autre de ses caravanes à cette oasis ou encore cette même caravane pourra encore marquer des points en reliant une autre oasis.

3- Fermer un territoire : On entend par territoire une surface fermée par une et une seule caravane avec l’aide éventuel des bords du plateau de jeu et des surfaces montagneuses. En outre pour gagner des points il ne faut pas qu’il y ait de chameaux à l’intérieur du territoire. Si le territoire contient des points d’eau le joueur les récupère, si le territoire contient une oasis le joueur gagne le marqueur oasis correspondant et en plus à la fin de la partie, il marquera 1 point pour chaque hexagone vide.

4- Avoir les caravanes les plus longues:. A la fin de la partie, on regarde pour chaque couleur de chameau quel joueur a la caravane la plus importante, il suffit de compter les chameaux, il gagnera 10 points et en cas d’égalité le gain sera de 5 points pour tous.

Fin de la partie: Lorsque la réserve de chameaux d’une couleur est épuisée.

Mon opinion
Durch die Wuste est un autre jeu de pose de Reiner Knizia qui m’avait déjà comblé avec Samuraï et surtout la perle qu’est Euphrat und Tigris. Trois jeux de pose certes, mais aux mécanismes si différents que les trois méritent d’être achetés, aucun ne faisant double emploi avec un autre.
Dans Durch die Wuste la difficulté vient du nombre de façon de gagner des points, tout paraît accessible, à portée de main, mais il va pourtant falloir se concentrer sur les objectifs les plus profitables. Il faudra faire la part des choses entre le gain immédiat et les possibilités de développements futurs.
Beaucoup ont comparé ce jeu au go du fait qu’on y retrouve les notions d’expansion, de blocage, d’enferment et de coups quasi obligatoires en fin de partie. J’ai longtemps trouvé cette comparaison très exagérée jusqu’au moment où j'ai joué à 2 joueurs. Et je dois dire que dans cette configuration la filiation avec le go est plus évidente. En fait Durch die Wuste prend toute sa saveur lorsqu’il est joué à deux, avec davantage de joueurs cela reste très prenant mais il perd de sa pureté. Avec l’augmentation du nombre de joueur on passe d’un jeu abstrait et tendu, à un jeu de société familial rapide et agréable.
Tout le monde sait que les jeux aux règles les plus simples sont également les plus difficiles à maîtriser. On en a un illustration flagrante avec Durch die Wuste et le go. On prend le go et on multiplie les contraintes (poser à la suite d’un chameau de même couleur et du même joueur), les façons de gagner des points (occupation de cases de valeur différentes, territoires fermés, principe de collection pour les caravanes les plus longues) et finalement on obtient un jeu bien plus facile que le go ! En tout cas j’ai quant à moi toujours beaucoup de plaisir à jouer à Durch die Wuste et ce, dans toutes les configurations. Un futur classique.

25/08/05

RATING: 8.9
[August 2017 - 8.9]
What an awesome little game that plays great at two players, all the way up to five. There's just a few ways to score points, which would seem easy, but the board quickly tightens up and gives you some good area-control decisions. There are many instances where you think you've come up with a strategy to easily win, but the game and your opponents check you. Really well balanced and games tend to run just 25 minutes.

As most have pointed out, the only con, is the pastel-colored camels... especially the green and blue are hard to tell apart in dim lighting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a game where at first I didn't like it, I bought it then traded it, then bought it again, then traded it again and subsequently bought it again. This time though it'll stay for good because I've finally warmed up to and discovered just what an amazing game it is. Every turn all you have to do is pick the colors of two camels and add them to an existing line...Yet what to pick and where to place them is stressful. The game state moves slow so you can see your opponent's plans and you want to mess with them. But by messing with them you might hurt yourself in the long run by neglecting your own pieces. There's internal conflict between growing caravans slow but evenly compared to one caravan fast. I have come to appreciate the simple nature of the rules but the complex decision make the game forces you to make. Simply brilliant.

I found the pastel camels garish at first, but somehow they really grew on me. :-) The only reason that I don't give it an 8 or 9 is that it is hard for me to get this one to the table for some reason. Maybe it's a bit too dry or analytical for my tastes... the fun factor of other Knizia games is higher. This is still a brilliant game, though.

RATING: 8

The setup is the only real hump in the game.  For such a simple and fast game the setup is just a twinge long.  I suppose the real issue is that I play this mostly with newbie gamers and so unless I've had a chance to review the setup I'm not too motivated to pull it out and stumble though all the components while newbies await their next gaming experience.  Nothing insurmountable, but it does get kept on the shelf because of this reason at times.

RATING: 6
Through the Desert is an interesting version of a territory control game.  Players place camels on the board in caravans of like color in order to gain points through surrounding or touching oases and squares with points.

It's fun, but there's only one possible action during play (place camels), so I can see it getting dull over time unless you really enjoy abstract strategy games such as "Go."  Personally, I prefer game play with varied options, so this game won't be on my favorites list.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
["Tile" placement, Spatial] {4P:8, 3P:8, 2P:8} {FFG Reprint May 2005. With plastic pastel camels. Without plastic palm trees. Good thing little plastic palm trees aren't that hard to find... CORRECTION: Chris Petersen says WITH little plastic palm trees, so we lose nothing! *dance of joy*} Reiner Knizia's "Game of Life" where there are so many things to do but only two camels a turn to do them with. Yeah, that sounds weird. If anyone ever asks me what the essence of German gaming is, I'd pull this game out to illustrate. Very simple mechanisms, no luck, very tough decisions, huge turn angst and concepts that are easy to grasp... all in a fun 30-minute game. There's always a lot of table talk going on, with players pointing out where each play should go to block off someone else other than themselves, or who's got the longest caravan, or who's in the lead. The presentation of the game, with its plastic camels and desert terrain, is no slouch either. No, the theme-to-mechanism ties aren't robust, but in a game this simple I don't think they could be regardless if it's desert oases or putting greens. This is pure game, and it's very, very good. There's not enough depth here to make it insanely great, but as gateway games go, Through the Desert is always one of the best places to start. Highly recommended.

RATING: 7
Played two games with Charles (red beard), and both times I scored about 80-85 and he scored 150 the first time and 130 the second.  Fun game and wouldn't mind playing it again ... but with three players, not just two.  Strategy hints:  Set up caravan leaders roughly one per every 1/5 of the playing field and as close to oases as possible; game is more about area control than anything, so try to aim for that instead of point counters; and try to connect caravan routes to each oasis for 5 points each.

RATING: 8
Knizia borrows a few elements from a number of classic designs and makes a very enjoyable area enclosure / network game. It's easy game to teach but deep enough to pull out for a mid-weight filler. Through the Desert has so many placement options, but the limit of two pieces a turn keeps things snappy and interesting. Love the look of the camels, though under low light it can be difficult to tell differences in colour. Overall, TtD works very well, is wonderfully straightforward in execution and yet it is quite a tense and confrontational game. At times, a bit mean! (But the 'good' kind of mean').

Want to play more. And with more players.

RATING: 9
Acquired December 2005; first played January 2006. Hard to beat this game for 5 players. No luck involved. Pure strategy and tactics play. Tense game, if you are really into it -- always wanting to place more than 2 camels per turn. Must adjust your plans to accommodate other players' moves. Can be frustrating, but everyone has the same chance to win, so usually everyone has the same frustration. Generates some conversation during, and mostly after, the game. Must remember to keep all the scoring options in mind throughout the game.

May 2009 -- We haven't played this game in quite a while, but I still think it is one of the better games I've playd.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I strongly prefer games with at least a dollop of luck or randomness thrown in somewhere rather than luck-free games of pure strategy.  I enjoy having to adjust to unpredictable game situations, and usually some random element is necessary to give me this feeling.  Durch die Wüste gives me this feeling without containing any random elements beyond setting up the board and choosing a start player.  The choices the game presents are so open and balanced that it's difficult to predict what my opponents will do in many cases.  That player-created chaos provides the tension and uncertainty I love with no need for true randomness.

Reiner Knizia has expertly tweaked the rules so that the game is perfectly balanced with 2, 3, 4, or 5 players (although I personally would recommend 2-4 only).  I wish more designers would follow his lead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
3 player only (for me).

Alterations: Open information 'Water holes'; Open scores (and well organized for easy reading); No tie-breaker, if there is one.

Touchstones: Manipulation of ambiguity

---

This game gains importance with me. The ticking clock on all of the scoring opportunities reshapes how the board might develop as I watch. Also, one's thoughts seem more exposed when these chains each have potential from opposite ends; none of the safety of having your back against the wall. 

Initial placements above and below the trees/oasis destabilized the area to a much greater degree than just off to the sides. If below, one can expect the 5 points, a waterhole or two, a few hexes, and a spoiler to clip any land grabs from either side. If above, one could capture the tree and a few hexes and build towards the 'island' with a chance at longest caravan. In either case it rushes any decision by opponents leaving one free to work elsewhere.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2
I simply don't see makes this game so great.  I love Reiner Knizia's other tile games, (T&E and Samuria) but this one I just don't get.  First I hate all the colors.  The game is just painful to look at.  It's hard to tell who the cammels belong to.(Should have used different shapes.)  So much of the game depends on the starting placement of the riders.  If even one player does not know what they are doing they can easily give another player a chance to enclose a large part of the board.  With so many ways to score, this is not a good game for anyone but hardcore gamers.   The players must constantly keep track of the score of other players so they can try to stop them from scoring.  However using a cammel to stop someone from scoring usually meens sacrificing points of your own or allowing yet another player to score.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A Knizia design that reminds me of Africa. Players race around trying to collect VP by running their camel caravans through oases and touching palm trees.

With 5 players it is chaotic, as each player has 5x caravans. Each turn a player can play up to two camels, and that's it. They can be both placed in the same caravan or split between two. Since only one camel can be placed in each hex, this is a race to grab as many VP before you're cut off, and the feel is similar to Samarkand. The only restriction is that each caravan is of a certain color, so camels cannot be placed that would merge two caravans of the same color.

Additional VP are awarded for encircling empty hexes with camels from a number of caravans belonging to a single player, in a manner like Go. Finally, bonus VP are awarded from getting the most camels out for each color of caravan.

It's fast and furious. However, it is clearly an abstract with the theme pasted on. So, it plays more like multi-player Go than anything else. Tempo is key, but with 5 players, that will get chaotic, so there are lots of things to keep track of. It's a nice filler, but not more than that. Once an evening is enough for me.

RATING: 8
The idea behind the game is interesting. Each player has a group of camels and you have to carefully construct camel chains to reach waterholes and oasises. 

The game can get pretty busy with lots of players and your routes will get cut off quick. You have to watch and make sure you can get where you need to get.

It's a fun game with cute, colorful camel pieces. That's one of the fun thigns for me about the game.

We usually play with the waterholes turned upside down to hide their value and add a little luck to the game.

RATING: 6
Knizia in a good form.  It's all about prioritization.  There are plenty of things you wanna do and only 2 actions in your turn :)  There's a lot of TENSION when you play this.

Dynamic, smart game with proper amount of decisions packed in a short playing time.

(On the downside: the game end can really be frustrating - that's why you could try a house rule of allowing the completion of actions by all players up to the starting player (thus each player gets the same no. of turns).  I know the sudden end is a part of the game and is what you have to carefully watch out for and have to definitely be prepared for, BUT anyway it sometimes creates too much of randomness and contributes to kingmaking.  The more players the more painful the problem.  5-player game is probably too much out of control anyway...)

EDIT:  Not a bad game at all, but not really that exciting now when I got to know sooo many games since I played TtD for the first time...  And I've got no time (and storage space) for just decent games when I struggle with time (and storage space) for great/brilliant ones...

Sold.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A great accessible & scalable mid-weight elegant easy to teach gateway abstract game that has a brisk pace and short game length making it perfect for weeknights when brain power and time are limited. 

Now not to say there is no depth as 4 different ways to score points ensures you have lots of things to keep track of on the board, the enclosure tactic is by far the most lucrative if you can pull it off without getting blocked. Amazing that you are only putting 2 camels on the board on your turn(similarity to GO, play one piece) but yet lots to think about and analyse and with no real AP either so it plays wonderfully fast. You could almost call this a filler except there is much more depth than games in that genre.

Will be buying this for all reasons stated above and to have another 'no luck game'. Will update after more plays with all player numbers...

[u]UPDATE[/u]: upped the rating to a solid 8 for this game right there with Samurai. They are close to the same weight, funny this one had a little more than I expected(probably the camels mislead me)and Samurai had a little less, regardless both "must own" games in IMO. This game really shines with 2 as there are more opportunities to be had and to be lost, it also feels a little more "Go" like and is more vicious as screwage is absolutely crucial to winning. 

Still excellent at any number as I have played 2 right to 5 now and despite what some people say 5 was great once one caravan leader was removed from each player as suggested in the rules. Enclosures are a little harder to do at higher numbers so racing to the water and the Oasis's becomes a little more important. My only knock is the set-up is a little long compared to the game length, this just means you should play 2 or 3 games since it is so good and so fast.

Another true classic from the Doc and quite addicting in the discovery stage, thank-you yet again Reiner (no wonder he has a cult :) ).

Initial Rating: 6.0 (March 2012)

It’s been years since I last played Through the Desert. I remember I never liked it very much but the game looks great on the table. The components are very nice. I bought the game recently so I hope to play it again soon. When I do, I will come back to make a proper comment.

Current Rating: 6.0
[hr]
2017-01-31

I finally played the game again.

The theme is ok but Through the Desert is an abstract game so you don’t feel it.

The rules are very easy to explain, you can do it under 8 minutes. Playtime is around 45 minutes.

The game´s components are great looking.

The game´s decisions aren’t obvious but aren’t very hard either. You can play it very fast. I should like it more but for some reason I don’t. Perhaps I could appreciate it better as a two player game, but with lots of players your plans will be for naught and it becomes a tactics endeavor.

Bottom line, Through the Desert is not a game for me. It is light and quick but it looks great on the table. I already sold my copy of the game. It can be a good family game.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: 10
I can't get enough of this game! It's the best two-person game I've found. It's easy to learn the rules, and at first it seems like a fairly simple game, but as I play it more and more, I've come to realize how complex it really is. Every turn there are numerous things you need to do, but only two camels! Should I try to enclose, or should I aim for the oasis, or should I cut off my opponent, or should I...AHHHHH!!!!  Need more camels!

And I love the camels so much I like to take them out of their little baggies and play with them. CAMELS! They look delicious, don't they?  And look at the little faces of the camel riders!  It's my favorite game right now. I have to bribe my husband to play it with me AGAIN by promising to play Connect Four with him (he wins about 8 out of 10 Connect Four games).  So far we're tied at Through the Desert.  And, yes, we do keep track.

RATING: 8.5
I knew this was a pretty abstract game so I tried it out with a buddy of mine who likes games like Go. This was an instant hit and I immediately wanted to try it again. I really enjoy the desert "theme" even if it doesn't have much to do with the gameplay; it still adds considerably to the experience for me. The first time I ever saw the pastel camels I was really turned off, but they've grown on me...

I won this when my Duke Blue Devils won the bball championship and I got 2nd in the Thoughthammer contest (2010). Thanks Thoughthammer for adding such a gem to my collection!

RATING: 9
An extremely elegant and fun "tile" (camel) laying game.  In 45 minutes, players experience a full game narrative.  First, players are sniping at short range scoring opportunities.  As the game develops, players focus on strategic placement, seeking to secure area and points, and to hem in their opponents.  Finally, players balance the prior objectives with securing majorities for large caravans.

Both exceptionally simple and rich.

RATING: 8
On each of your turns you have a need for 10 camels, but you can only use 2. That is great.  Pastelca(ra)mels are really cool, but the colours of the camels are not easily distinguishable from each other when lights are not perfect. That's not so good. Add to that the camel riders and you may get lost on the board. Also a negative thing is that players most probably aren't really aiming at playing with all of their colours but are instead using maybe 1 or 2 colours just to screw others. In the end there may arise a situation where a player simply loses no matter what he does. Despite all these little negatives I think this is a really good and tactically deep game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A solid example of how chaos and strategy do not need to be opposites on a continuum. Managing what will be there on the following turns, and what you're willing to risk, where it's worth blocking opponents, is the heart of the game. I'm not entirely sure how, but this game takes a 2P spatial abstract game (which I usually hate), and keeps that feel with multiple player, without being AP-inducing. Again, Knizia impresses me.

RATING: 9
This is rated as a two-player game, I like it somewhat less with more.  I do like games with a spatial element and where you place things very much matters in this game (And as in many abstract games initial placement is also crucial).  You balance tactical goals of grabbing victory chips with mid-term goals of connecting to a limited number of oasis's along with longer term goals of encompassing territory and even having the longest caravan in as many colors as possible.  Balancing that continually is what makes the game so interesting especially considering that you only get to play two camels on each turn.  There is also a limited supply of each color so, especially in a two-player game, you can factor in choices that hinder what your opponent can do (i.e. Take camels in a color such that he/she can no longer finish encircling a territory or get a longer caravan in that color, etc.).  This is one of my wife's favorite games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Only problem is that initial placement can be quite important strategically, and newcomers will have no sense of how to strategize. Short-term tactics on the other hand are fairly easy to pick up.

RATING: 6.5
This is a great gateway game that has a lot of depth.  It is so simple and attractive that people want to give it a try, and the limited options make it a game that newbies can feel competent in from the very beginning.  There is a great balance in this game, because while game play itself is very simple, its multiple scoring options generate many different strategic and tactical decisions. It's a really good game for 2-5 people that can bridge the gap between gamer and "regular" people.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Like many of Knizia's abstracts, it's simple, it has only a few rules to remember, yet it has the depth of multiple variable calculus. And mastering the game might require that level of education. Half the game is making the best of your initial camel placement - the other half is making those placements score as much as you planned to.

Few abstracts for more than two players really work out, but this one does. That it is disguised with a desert theme and pastel camels makes it work all the better with kids. I find that kids do well with abstracts, and this is a good one for keeping them engaged in the game (they cannot resist the camels!) while exercising their brain matter.

Wins (3 out of 6)
German version bought. 
Give it the same rating as Carcassonne. My favourite game so far. (have to properly play caylus, puerto Rico, Power Grid and Tigris and euphrates yet)
It's better than carcassonne because it cancel's out luck factor of tile draw. (There are house rules in carcassonne that allow you to allways have two tiles in stock) It is worse because another play can make it possible in the end game to take two camels he doesn't need just to give someone else the oppertunity to give her the longest caravan and end the game. Allthough the  player doesn't have a chance to win. In other words because games are close,it is possible for the loser to determine the winner in one or two turns.
This means that if i come along other games that know how to deal with this problem the score isn't likely to go up.
Because of it's easy rules and quick play. It is highly recommended as a filler. I think it's better than ticket to ride. I am a bit surprised that this isn't mentioned as much  as a gateway game.
Highly recommended.
Oh yes components are brilliant. Maybe the colour of the camels aren't ideal for those suffering of colourblindness. 

Update
Confirmed as a top game after play with family.
Recommended to all. There is enough depth in it. Has great replayvalue.

Played with one twelve year old and a thirty year old ;twelve year old won because we were to nice :) And when we saw the danger it was to late.
Rules and different options are easy to grasp.

Update. This game gets the first nine. I can't stop thinking about it.
:)
Update 04-04-07. With four players this game is too fast and you loose a lot of control.
It is a 10 as a two player game. 
A 8.4 as a 3Pgame. And a 7.5 as a 4Pgame
With more players I prefer Carcassonne.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Deep decisions at every move, and yet it never feels too heavy.  Four ways to score points, a constantly changing board, and a total lack of luck means you've got to stay on your toes the entire game.  A favorite in our group.  I just taught this to my 10-year-old sister, and she likes it too.

This is one of those games that provokes post-game analysis:  "If you hadn't blocked me from that last Oasis, she wouldn't have won!"  And all this folds nicely into a 30 or 40 minute game.  Yes, I'll play "Through the Desert" once or twice a week if I can.

People talk about "Ticket to Ride" being a "good gateway game", but why not this?  This has a similar objective (link chains of objects to reach your goals), but has infinitely greater depth, simpler rules, and more charming bits!  Nobody doesn't love pastel camels.

I can think of at least one way the game could be better, though.  They should have included a variable board, or moveable obstacles, that would make each match truly different and prevent fixed placement strategies.

RATING: 8
Great strategy and tactics placement game.  There are four scoring categories (water, oasis, encircled regions, long caravans); this makes your strategy evolves during a single game, which is pleasant.  Sometimes feels like Go: the camels do not move and you can score by encircling regions, which creates a strong pressure to build territory (while trying to reach water holes and oasis at the same time).  My 6 year old likes it very much, but it is also a good game for gamers.

RATING: 7
Many have compared this game to Go, but I don't see the comparison. Go takes forever to play and strategies for playing are quite mysterious (despite simple rules). Oddly, Through the Desert has more rules, but it is easier to play and appears to have straightforward strategies. Further, the theme (though ridiculous) makes the game more approachable than Go.

That said, the game is still very dry and moves seem fairly obvious to me. Further the S/O does not like pure abstract games so this does not get much love at our house.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First of all, do not eat the camels! I know they look tasty, but they're only plastic.

Through the Desert is a clever and distinctive near-abstract from the heyday of the grandmaster Knizia. While I disagree with the description "multiplayer Go", the purity of this design shines through.

Through the Desert is a network building game, not all that far removed from Ticket to Ride, really (or, rather, I wonder how much TtD inspired TtR). Unlike TtR, however, the routes are built on a hex grid instead of preplanned, More significantly, players are not trying to connect two randomly selected points; they have to navigate a myriad of scoring opportunities.

I think the only thing separating Through the Desert from true greatness is the slightly uninspiring scoring. It's well-balanced, but a little flat and workmanlike. I think a little more Ra or Tigris & Euphrates in its scoring DNA would give TtD the shot in the arm it needs to become one of the top tier of euros.

Nevertheless, for a half-hour game that's simple enough for children to play, there is plenty to think about, enough planning not to feel hollow, and enough player interaction to produce the occasional four-letter word. Now that FFG is reprinting TtD again, I need to fill this hole in my collection.

Durch die Wüste ist so perfekt ausbalanciert und mathematisch optimal konstruiert, dass es den Anschein hat, als habe ein fehlerfreies Programm das Spiel entwickelt.

A simple, clever game, with short playing-time, turn angst and meaningful decisions. Only drawback is the set-up where you always have to count camels, sort camels and remove camels. Number of camels depends on the number of players, so I haven´t found a good way to sort the camels in the box. It´s a small complaint, but a fiddly set-up feels more fiddly in a filler like this. I guess it´s the price we pay for good scalability, which this game has. Anyway, a really good game that I´m always happy to play.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Game in a Nutshell: Alternate playing camels in order to claim area and score points in several different ways.

Played as: 2,3,4,5 players

Pros: I love the contention. It's hard not to make a move that doesn't seem like you're after one person or another.

Cons: I feel it takes a few plays to generate interest rather then loving it the first time. Partially because the theme does nothing to excite one past seeing pastel camels and also because without the theme, it's still just a bland idea for an abstract that works as well as it does because you can't help but step on each others toes.

Other: For the most part, this game has been outdone by Kingdom Builder which has a similar feel but much more variety. The one thing this has over that is a simpler entry point and a bit more interaction. I didn't like playing this with two or five players for opposite reasons: With two there was surprisingly less direct competition and more straight forward decisions, and with five the game became too crowded and nearly unmanageable.

Best Use: Still unsure here (due to the love-it or hate-it reaction it's invoked so far) but I'd say best for 3-4 players with more of a gateway crowd.

RATING: 9.5
My #25.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
After ~1 play.  Weird.  A connection game with chrome (arbitrarily desert-caravan-themed, no less). The comparisons with Go are wrong.  A better comparison is Y.  It's solid mechanics-wise, but if I'm gonna play a connection game, it's gonna be Hex, or Y, or Star, or Gonnect, or Projex, or any of a large number of far superior games.

It's redeeming quality is that it introduces Euro-gamer-types to the joys of connection games, of which they are usually ignorant because they tend to overlook abstracts while being distracted with shiny plastic/wooden things.

The only reason this gets a 5 (which is not a bad rating for me) is that it is essentially a connection game, a mechanic I adore.  There is something off-putting about the meaningless theme and pointless chrome.  It doesn't sit well on this kind of game, maybe because I am so used to the "purity" of other connection games.

RATING: 5
La verdad es que es un juego que nunca me ha llamado mucho. Lo he visto en las mesas de muchos jugones unas cuantas veces, desde hace 2 o 3 años cuando Edge se lanzó a la aventura de traducir juegos de mesa aquí en España. Los materiales no son malos, pero como ya comentamos, los colores son matadores. El juego en sí no me pareció malo. Las estrategias pueden ser muy diversas e interesantes, algo parecido a los que ocurre con Pingüinos y Cia. Hay más "chicha" de lo que aparentemente se ve y no es juego escesivamente complicado o díficil  para jugadores nóveles. Está claro que como todo juego de vecino, necesita más partidas para sacarle todo su potencial. Knizia es un buen diseñador de juegos, sobre todo en cuanto a juegos de enfrentamiento directo por puntos se refiere. Me encanta su juego para 2 "Scarab Lords" y éste, aunque poco tiene que ver, lleva el enfrentamiento de recolecta de puntos contra un grupo más amplio de jugadores. Aún así lo veo demasiado límitado a la hora de evitar que los jodepartidas hagan su trabajo. El hecho de que se termine el juego cuando la reserva de uno de los colores de camellos se acabe, facilita que si alguién quiere joder la partida no por táctica, sino por jodienda al resto de jugadores, no se pueda evitar.

RATING: 10
Plastic camels, indeed. I don't play this as much as I used to (unlike [gameid=3], I did play Through the Desert before I started logging plays). This is a shame, because it has excellent replayability due to random elements of the setup. I don't like the Go comparisons, which I feel are superficial. Making territory is only one aspect of this multi-faceted game. There are very few 2-player abstracts which also work well with more players, and this is one of them. The two-moves-(in this case, camels)-per-turn idea is one which I'd like to try in several other games.

The game just feels great: the turn angst is finely tuned, and you have to pay attention to all areas of scoring. So it overcomes my preference for games where you don't have to do a lot of counting to find out who won. Once upon a time, I rated this a 10. I don't know why I ever lowered it.

[size=10][u][i][b]r{Initial Thoughts}r (after 5 plays):[/u][/b][/i][/size]
* Beautifully produced abstract, complete with pastel camels
* Gameplay is simple (place two camels) but decisions are deliciously difficult
* Typical Knizia, in that scoring is math-based and dependent upon majorities
* More players at the table greatly increase the amount of player screwage
* Bit of a learning curve, as newbies may not see the strategies of enclosing/blocking
* Good with any number of players and Knizia fans
* Initial rating: [b]8/10[/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Neat area control game where you build herds of different-colored camels; the multiple methods for acquiring points (placing camels near oases, placing camels on point chips, enclosing areas of the board, and having the largest herd of any one color) and extremely limited move options (you can only place 2 camels per turn, far fewer than you'd like to) make the game very interesting.  The game is competitive (in that you can block other players), but not aggressive (in that you can't destroy other players pieces).  Rules are simple to explain and understand; possibly a good game for non-gamers who are interested in gaming.

RATING: 10
A great game. It was interesting to hear Dr. Knizia himself speak of this game with such fondness. I agree that it is one of his very best games. Works well with two players, even better with three, and perhaps best with four. By the way, Durch is mostly not Go-like: it's multi-player; there's no capturing; enclosing territory is just one of four ways to score (two tactical, two strategic); each turn you place two camels; it's much shorter, and no where near as deep. RATING: 5.5
I liked the idea of the game, but there is too much of an "anti-" streak to this game. By this I mean, you make actions specifically intended to hurt another player without boosting your own position. Games like this enable stubborn players or those who have no hope of winning to "mess with" the game on a level I just don't fully like.  For example, one person could do very well, and be trailed by two other players.  A final player could be doing horribly, and then can decide just to mess with the leader. They can cripple the chances for a win by that leader, only by completely ruling themselves out of the game. I guess I like games which reward playing to win over playing to ruin another player's chances of winning.

RATING: 7.5
A solid almost-abstract. Has Knizia's trademark focus on balancing your scoring (in this case keeping your caravans similar lengths so you can sweep up the 10 point length bonuses at game-end). The production is fine, but the board is really ambiguous in the shaded regions of the board - is this a space or not? Semi-cut-throat yet almost Zen-like at the same time.

RATING: 8.3
Wow! Je découvre beaucoup trop tardivement l'univers des jeux de société, et, au sommet de celui-ci, la trilogie des jeux de positionnement de Reiner Knizia. 

Si Tigris & Euphrates demeure au sommet de mon classement depuis sa découverte en 2012, Samurai et Through the Desert, découverts respectivement en février et avril 2014, sont instantanément des jeux qui s'accaparent d'une place dans mon "Top 10".

Joué pour le moment à deux reprises à trois joueurs (deux parties dans la même soirée contre les mêmes joueurs), je vois mal comment nous pourrons résister à l'idée d'y rejouer lorsque nous nous reverrons!

Tout le monde essaie de jouer des coups un peu salauds, qui nuiront aux autres; mais, oh malheur, seuls deux petites actions peuvent être jouées à chaque tour!

Un délice intellectuel! =)

EDIT: J'ai rejoué depuis à 2, 3 et 4 joueurs, et il est nettement plus intéressant à 3 joueurs.

RATING: 7.2
A pretty thinky abstract. While you place only two pieces per turn, the game awards points to you in three distinct ways - enclosing areas, a race to certain point-gaining hexes, and forming the longest chains of camels. The cleverness and subtlety is excellent, however it's AP-inducing as well. Still a good game, but I prefer to play it by instincts rather than configuring the one best move.

RATING: 6
Nice little abstract that is like a complex Lost Cities mated with Ticket to Ride.  Where LC has 4 color-coded cards that get played in ascending numerical order, this has 5 pastel colored camel sets that get added to a "caravan."  Points are scored when the caravans cross a numbered water marker, hit up against an oasis marker or block off a section of the board.  

This isn't a game to take overly seriously.  I like how points are accumulated as the game goes along.  I also like that it's quick to play and easy to teach (works just fine with my 10 and 14 y.o. daughters).  I don't like how crowded the board gets.  So far I've only played two-person with each of my daughters, and that already produces a vaguely constipated gaming experience.  Three-player would be similar to two because all of the board gets used, rather than just most.  Anything more than three-player, I wouldn't want to play.

RATING: 7
Good game from Knizia. Each turn, play two colored camels (from five different colors) to earn points by : 1) connecting to point score markers, 2) connect to an oasis, 3) seal off areas, 4)get majority in camel colors.  A lot of player interaction determines the outcome of the game (an opponent might shut you out of an oasis, prevent you from forming walls). Being an older Knizia game (like Samurai), this game hasn't been played lately.

RATING: 5
Many people have described Through The Desert as Go With Camels, but a more apt description would be Hey! That's My Oasis!

Compared to Hey! That's My Fish!, TtD has a more complex scoring system and a set number of units (5 caravan leaders instead of 2-4 penguins). The fact that camels are placed on the tiles rather than the tiles being lifted away is the main gameplay digference. These changes are enough to elevate TtD from the kids game of H!TMF! to a more family-weight experience. Neither are particularly energetic plays, but the increased complexity of TtD makes it the better game.

RATING: 7
Miserable colour scheme and a faint theme (to the point of superfluousness), but when you get down to it a great, thought-provoking abstract. Like Ingenious, Knizia's other great hex game, Through The Desert mandates that you keep an eye on several different things all at the same time, but with this one you can win on individual strengths, rather than spreading your resources. The player-determined endpoint is the vital rule that keeps you on your toes. I'm not happy about the gameplay/setup time ratio, though.

One of those unusual games that always makes you wish you were playing with a different number of players to the ones you currently are playing with.

[i]First copy traded for Key Harvest[/i]
[i]Second copy traded for Dominion: Guilds[/i]

RATING: 9
50+ plays. Classic multiple-race game that plays smoothly and fast, and scales beautifully across different numbers of players. This has seen quite a bit of play in the family arena due to its rule simplicity and 30 minute playing time, players being attracted to the continual pressure of which threat they most need to react to each turn with only 2 actions at their disposal. There's a strategic vs tactical tradeoff to be made throughout - whether to concentrate on building a colour for the end-game bonus vs one-off points in a colour that won't get you an end-game bonus. It's a lot of punch for an otherwise shortish game, and always very enjoyable.

RATING: 4
What I like:
- Very cute components
- Quick (40 minutes for 4p learning game)
- Typical Knizia variety of scoring

What I don't like:
- Feels a lot like Go
- I don't like area control games
- I don't like games this cutthroat

It's not quite as abstract as some people say, but clearly the theme isn't strong. It is a bit daunting at first, trying to figure out the 20 chains on the board (with 4p). You always feel like there are 10 things you want to do, but can only place 2 camels per turn.

It can (and probably should be) be very cutthroat. I would put the rules weight around 2.2 and the depth around 3.0 for a weight of 2.6.

RATING: 9
Some have said this game reminds them of Go and I agree to an extent.  It is shorter and lighter than Go, just more fun (in my opinion of course).  This game fits a very specific niche that few other games fit.  It doesn't take long to play and it doesn't take up a lot of room.  The compact box makes it very portable.  It's easy to teach and it's a game you can play with a variety of people: your wife, your girlfriend (hopefully not both), your parents, your kids, etc.  Its a perfect strategy game when you don't have 2 hours to spare.  But masked beneath the simplicity and fun is a game with a lot of tactical and strategic depth, yet very little "weight".  Repeated play definitely results in greater skill.

The only con I can see is that the set up time is a tad long for how long the game takes.  A 2 player game is over in 15 to 20 minutes but the game takes 5 minutes to set up.  If a game takes me 5 minutes to set up, I would like to get a good hour out of it, but that may be just a personal thing.  BTW, you can expect a 4 player game to take about 45 minutes.

UPDATE:  I actually got to play with 5 players and it was a blast.  You have to make sure you follow the specific 5 player rule where each player reduces his caravan leaders by one color and each player must eliminate a DIFFERENT color.  A surprisingly good 5 player game.

RATING: 8
This is a perfect information game that combines area fencing with picking up victory point chits from the board.  It seems like to win, you have to do both instead of concentrating on one or the other.  There can be a lot of tension in the game, as blocking off your opponents is a key strategy.  There's also a bonus for longest chain of a color, forcing you to pay attention to your opponents even more.  Knizia has you juggling a lot of different goals here, which makes to a fun and tense game experience.

RATING: 9
Beautiful. Elegant. Easy to learn, but with a lot of different possible strategies and tactics. It plays fast, as you always want to do a lot of stuff before others make that impossible. Can be quite cutthroat. I love how the turns are so simple (place two camels on the board. that's it.), yet with every turn the game becomes more exciting as you see more opportunities, for yourself and for your opponents. It might not be the deepest, most interesting game in my collection, but it just might be the most fun to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
For some reason this game makes me thirsty for victory points. I think it is because the vps are visually and mentally easy to obtain: picking up waterholes and hitting the oasis are close to your camel trains and surrounding territories can scoop a nice boost in scoring. There is a gaming cliche of "wanting to do more in your turn than allowed" and for me only this game justifies that feeling because of the ease of "seeing" the vps but also because of the mini races to get to them before being blocked off by opponents' camels (or because of the one vacant space between same colour camel rule). With only 2 camels to place in a turn and your opponents trying for the same goals gives this dry exercise some necessary tension, as does the countdown of the game end when a colour group of camels is used up. I think I prefer 2-3 players rather than more as it can get visually confusing and you are really stretch to try to achieve your little objectives. Not a big fan of open information games as I prefer a small element of secrecy and luck but here at least part of the scores become a secret stack, and the cute camels helps the theme making it alittle warmer than most cold abstracts. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
-

I can't say enough good things about this game - utterly brilliant. One of the most elegant game designs of all time; it is just perfect, nothing more, nothing less. One of my all-time favorites. TtD is luck free and is a complete and perfect information game. You are the master of your destiny. Its rules are simple and straight forward, but it is always a nail-biter. Tense and highly tactical, it engrosses you to the very end. The variety of ways one can win victory points adds to the beauty of it. Infinite replayability value.

The theme is certainly dry, but that is not a detractor, at least for me. It is an abstract strategy game after all. The only negative thing I can say about TtD is that with 5 players there is less control than I would like.

All in all, a must own by gamers and non-gamers who enjoy light-middle weight strategy games.

Among Knizia's top 5 game designs (along with: Tigris & Euphrates, Amun-Re, Samurai, and Taj Mahal).

See my session report if interested:
http://boardgamegeek.com/article/686501#686501

RATING: 7
 A tile laying game of sorts, except that the tiles are tiny colored plastic camels. You're trying to spread multiple caravans of different colors to encircle areas, grab watering holes, and connect to oases, while your opponents are all doing the same, with your different caravans of the same color being forbidden to touch. It's a really clustered board, and moves sorta slow and fast at the same time, AND I LOVE IT! Samurai and Ra are games I feel I don't have a great grasp on, with Samurai still in a nebulous state as regards my feelings towards it, and Colossal is simple and quick in a way that I'm not thrilled about, but this is like a tile-laying game that in a pattern forming/mathy sort of way tickles me just like Modern Art. The only problem with the game itself is that the camel colors are all soft pastels, and many of them can be confused with another color (there is a pastel green and a neon green camel color). 

Overall I think I might have to rank this game super high, maybe even downgrade Modern Art in comparison. This game (and my victory in it; winning games on my first play often is indicative of my Spirit Damon being by my side) is really just great and I might have to break my spending moratorium if it gets the new printing that is due sometime soon.

RATING: N/A
I'm going to start things off by saying I don't play games for the theme any more than I drink beer for the bubbles. I think theme is (at its best) a source for vocabulary in order to explain the rules, and that's pretty much it. I don't find theme immersive in the slightest, and I don't think that all games ought to engage some topic for educational purposes. They're certainly welcome to, and I don't think that they can't or shouldn't, but rather I don't think "thematic coherence" is a non-negotiable quality that all games ought to aspire to. Adding "theme" often means adding rules and complexity purely in service of some kind of watered-down RPG fix. I also find that as "Realism" increases, so too do the number of rules required to support it. 

So that Through the Desert is an abstract game is not a problem -- there's nothing inherently wrong with an abstract game. That often means that the Game Problem is distilled to its essence, there's nothing distracting about it, and people can get right to competing within the constraints of that Game Problem. Through the Desert is a fairly simple territorial game that sees players drawing from a common pool of player pieces. This is a cool game clock that can be manipulated, and I am pretty partial to games where tempo management is one of the chief problems players have to work out. The camel pieces are used to create long chains that connect oases, or section off areas, or help to gather point tokens. And that's really it. On your turn, place two camels of any color and they can be in service of several different scoring or blocking goals. 

Overall, my bias: some people game because they want a story -- I'll read a story if I want a story. I game because I want a play-like structure to share with someone else and compete over/within. My wife frequently calls this her favorite game, and that's lucky for me. It's a really enjoyable abstract.

RATING: 7.2
After one 2-player game: Ooooh!  Pink camels!  Ok, ignoring the pink camels, this game is great fun for two, and I expect it's an even better brain battle for three or more.  Play offensively, play defensively, or try to grab your own while ignoring the other player's gains, tough choices.  I did well on my first play, and I'll definitely play it again.  Still, I wouldn't quite put it into the all-time classic category just yet; I have to try it with more players first.

RATING: 4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My girlfriend complains that this is 'yet another' game I've forced her to play that requires you to make great opening moves or suffer the consequences. There is some truth to that, poor initial placement of your caravan leaders can give you trouble later on. It's also the kind of game where you can suffer from analysis paralysis if you let it ... but you don't have to let it. Each turn you place two camels, either extending two caravans by one camel each, or extending one caravan by two camels. I like that there are multiple ways of scoring. There's a bit of a Go homage here, in that contiguous camels of the same color belong to a single caravan, and encircling or bordering off terrain gets you extra points. I like the uncertainty of the end game, but with a large number of players, I imagine that uncertainty would be very high. I look forward to another playing now that I understand the rules.

RATING: 6.5
This is great fun if you enjoy playing perfect-information abstracts. Each player plays down two camels a turn to add on to existing caravans in a bid to reach oases or to cordon off an area of the board. The rules are dead simple, yet the gameplay has a lot of depth.

RATING: 8
2006: 7. Initially loved this game - it was my first *real* board game purchase when I got into the hobby! - but got tiring with more plays.  Easy to learn, great presentation, but eventually becomes less-entertaining to non-gamers because there are no surprises.  Everyone's strategy is more or less clear on the board, there's nothing to hide or be sneaky about.  Great design, but get Samurai instead.

UPDATE 2014-02: 8. Brought this out to try with my non-gamer dad and step-mom, and they both really loved it! Attractive components and simple gameplay, very little hidden information. Both of them were making cutthroat competitive moves in only their second game. And enough time has gone by for the game to feel fresh again to me. Upgraded to an 8.

RATING: 6
Interesting. I enjoy the game a lot, but especially with two player games it can suffer from an imbalance of skill. There is no luck, and no hidden information. So with two players of uneven skill, the winner swiftly becomes apparent. Then the game becomes a chore, as the outcome is already decided. If this happens often enough between two opponents, the game loses its luster. This is true, to a lesser extent, with 3 and 4 players. I like the game nevertheless. I like the theme, even though it is criticized as being "dry". I enjoy the mechanics, which are mildly remiscent of Go and Twixt (for me, anyway).A warning though - this is not a gateway game, in my opinion. At least I have had ZERO luck with it as such. See, I've only listed two plays. I've exposed it to 4 different people, and talked to others about their experiences playing it. Same result - not a successful gateway game, that is, not unless a person is already a very gamer's-gamer sort of person.I think if you like some of Knizia's other designs, DdW is really worth a shot, however. You might really love it.

Abgepackt, Gespielt, Rules
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Another one of our very early purchases that I hadn't played in several years. Finally played it 8/09 online (Ludagora.net).

It's good, I suppose. But to me it's yet another abstract game that I'm not very good at playing!

--- Summary ---
Seed the board with bonus oasis chips (value 1-3), and trees. Players take turns placing a camel (with their color rider) in each of the 5 colors. Then rotate taking turns placing any 2 camels to extend your camel lines; game ends when any color camel is depleted. If you place camel onto oasis chip, take it. At end of game, score VP for chips collected, areas (including oasis chips) surrounded by your camel lines, and bonuses for longest camel line of each color. Most VP wins.

RATING: 5
I simply don't like abstract games.  There are a lot of choices and different ways to score and plan out your turns in this.  There are points for oasis', watering holes, and sectioning off parts of the map as well as opportunity to block your opponent and set the pace of the game.  These are a lot of decisions and if you devote the time to it you can get a lot of value out of this game.

I just don't like it enough to give it more than a single play unless someone really wants to play this.  I felt like the two-player game was just kind of uninteresting.  The 3- and 4-player games were increasingly more chaotic and still kind of uninteresting.  Abstracts just aren't for me, no matter how well designed it seems.

RATING: 8
I own the original German edition, "Durch Die Wuste" (the literal translation of which is "You're a two-hump chump and your momma has camel toe"), .  The English-language edition is "Through The Desert", not "Through The Dessert".  Also, typing up game reviews after several pints of beer is not recommended.  So let's start with those pastel-coloured camels.  A lot of people talk about how "cute" they are.  I say, how about normal-coloured camels with different-coloured saddles and trappings?  I don't need camels that look like they would taste like those minty-chocolate candies my grandparents used to have in their candy bowl.  In fact, I can vouch for the fact that they don't taste very good at all.  So now that the camels are taken care of, let me just say that this is actually a very enjoyable game.  It is also very simple to learn, and is one of my favourite games to teach to board game neophytes.  While I would like to be laying more than two camels at once (not in THAT way, pervert), it does, in typical Knizia style, add to a lot of the tension of the game.  In fact, there's enough going on here to keep my A.D.D. personality interested.  And speaking of staying interested, it's always entertaining to go back and read my own reviews after the beers wear off.  Game design: 4.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 3.5/5  Total: 8/10

RATING: 7
[Kosmos 'Durch die Wuste' 1998 edition] {Desert; connection; optimal 4-6p} A very good family abstract game. There aren't many good abstract games that can support up to 6p; if you like large-group abstract games, you should get a copy.

TtD is easy to learn and master. They won't lose their mind in the play for the simple rules and simple concept. Besides, attractiveness of the camels helps a lot.

There are many decisions in TtD. Players should to decide if they should attack/defend for each caravan, which chips to get, which longest caravan to make, etc. However, the decision is fairly easy to make because it's not very difficult to visualize 2 or 3 steps in advance. The stage of positioning riders is very important. Don't place camel riders mindlessly.

[Rule] I usually play TtD with the Kosmos rules.

RATING: 7.2
tl;dr: shows how good Reiner used to be

Simple rules, streamlined game play and interesting choices. These days Reiner seems to limit himself to the first 2, but when he made this game he remembered to put in the third, most important, ingredient.
I'm not going to say this is a sublime game or my favorite of his, but this clearly shows just how elegant and effective his designs from this period used to be.
So yeah, this is a very good design that hits a balance that few games do. I do enjoy playing it, but it is a bit one dimensional. For me Ticket to Ride is a game that is very close in it's simplicity but just a bit more fun than this one. Still, this one is certainly worth playing.

Initial rating: 7.2/10
BGG scale: 7/10

RATING: 6.4
[gameid=503] sees us taking on the mighty desert, nomads trying to survive in the wasteland - scoring points as we go along.

The game has some really cool camels and a theme that does the job, although it could be placed anywhere really. Still, glow-in-the-dark camels, that is pretty nifty.

Unfortunately the game is not so cool. Sure it is a [person=2] game and by some considered a classic, but it certainly didn't fall within our sphere of interest. It involves connecting oases, cutting off your opposing nomads or avoid being cut off.

I know the game by nature is an abstract, which rarely sits well with us, and area control is not a strong suit either. The game works by those standards, sure. Our games felt tense, despite, and you had to keep an eye on every finger - or rather camel.

That said, it will never be a favourite and has been traded off. The mechanics, theme and all-round gameplay just felt lacking.
