=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Through the Desert is an older classic. I didn't start gaming until well after it was released. And while I know people who own it, it's never been a hot game that people have pushed to play. I've always wanted to try it, but in a vague sort of way.

In the game, there are five colors of camels. Each player has a rider for each color (for some player counts this isn't true I think). The board is seeded with watering holes (point tiles) and oases (which you connect to for points). Players take turns seeding the board with their riders (which can't be placed adjacent to an oasis or any other player's piece). Once everyone has done their initial placement the regular game starts.

On a player's turn they place two camels on the board. The must place a camel adjacent to either their rider of the same color, or to a camel of the same color that forms a continuous chain of that color camel back to their rider. One player's chain of a color can never become adjacent to another player's chain of the same color (there can never be any ambiguity about whose chain of camels it is). The game ends when any one color of camels has been completely placed on the board.


If a player places a camel on a watering hole, they take that tile (1, 2 or 3 points). If they achieve adjacency to an oasis they get a 5 point tile (only the first instance of that player's chain being adjacent to a particular oasis counts). And if they section off an area where the boundaries of the area are only either the edges of the board and/or that player's color (and no other player's pieces are interior to that area) then they collect all the watering hole tokens in that area and each spot in that area will be a point for them at game end. Additionally, the player who has the longest chain of camels for a color will get the 10 point bonus for that color.

This game is very much a tactical area control game. I've recently discovered that I enjoy some older classic games that have a similar area control aspect, like Java and Mexica. This game hits many of the same notes for me. The rules are very simple but there's a lot to consider on your turn. I really enjoyed this one a lot.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I found this game to be superior in almost every way to [thing=8203][/thing]: there are more ways to score, thus, more things to be done, while the game is still easy to teach and to play. The play do last more (around 40 minutes), however, this is expected for a richer game. And also both share the same bad part: the ratio of time from set up to playing is far from great.

As for taste, [thing=503][/thing] almost fired [thing=8203][/thing], but this one will still have its place, since way more players in my group has it and also it is more portable (a big advantage when considering games to take to a game night), lighter and plays faster - so still has its niche.

The gameplay is pretty straightforward: first, every player will put one camel of his at a time. There are three rules for this placement: 1) you can't put a camel adjacent of other player's camel; 2) you can't put a camel adjacent of an oasis; 3) you can't put a camel above a score token. All other places are fine - you can put on the edge of the board, in the middle, whatever.

In his turn the player takes two camels (from different colors or the same) and puts then in the board, continuing a line of camels. If the camel is put above a points token or adjacent to an oasis, the player gains points. More points come if the player completely surround an area (1 point per hex in the area) with a single color of camels (and without any camels inside the area, even of the same player) and by having the biggest amount of camels of a color (10 points for each color). Camels of the same color cannot touch each other - there must be at least one hex of distance between them (and this can be use in a lot of ways to prevent other players to going some place); camels of different colors can be next to each other, but only one camel can be in every hex. And that is it for rules! 

Therefore, you will dispute for point tokens on the board, for spots adjacent of oasis, for the biggest amount of camels of each color and will try to close off areas to score even more points. The game ends as soon as a color of camels ends. The player with the most points wins!

So, [thing=503][/thing] has more to be done, but is still easy to learn and teach: take two camels, put them in the board. Done! Also, the board looks beautiful by the game end and the camels look just like candy!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Route-building game where each player has five routes. There are four ways for a route to score. I'm pretty sure Tom Vasel likes it because there are usually many things you can do on a given turn. That's less good for me, though it is helped by the fact that you can categorize your options according to which of your five routes would be expanded to accomplish them.

Due to the spatial nature of the game it is very difficult to calculate point deltas. I like this because it means I must play by gut, but I do kind of wonder whether it's less gut and more tunnel vision; i.e., if I'm simply ignoring some factors.

There's some not-so-good multiplayer conflict stuff of the "I can thwart him but it won't help me" sort.

A major reason I avoided this game for so long was that I didn't like the idea of the initial setup phase, a lengthy placement of initial camels where you can probably throw away a win if you do it badly. The strategy here is interesting to me though.

RATING: 6
From the award-winning game designer Reiner Knizia comes a game of strategy, patience, and cool plastic camels! The desert is still treacherous, mysterious, and without mercy. But for those willing to risk the dangers of the shifting, sun-baked sands, the desert holds riches beyond compare.
In Through the Desert, two to five players each control a tribe of nomads vying for control of the desert. By establishing caravans and taking over oases, the players gain points as their tribes increase in power.
Strategy is essential in deciding how and where to build your tribe's caravans. There are multiple ways to gain points and several ways to win. Should you try to build the longest caravan? Or should you dominate the desert's oases? Don't forget to keep an eye on your opponents' caravans, or you may find your own tribe cut off from valuable water holes.

Even the slightest randomization of watering holes and palm trees, along with different leader placements each game, give this wonder a vast amount of replayability. Like a more approachable version of Go, with an excellent balance of short-term tactics (palm trees, watering hole markers) and long term goals (areas, caravan bonuses). A good game with 3 or 4, but phenomenal with 2. One of the best 2 player games around.

Rating by Player Count:
2 Players- 9
3 Players- 8
4 Players- 8
5 Players- 7

RATING: 6.5
Every turn, it feels like you have to make a critical decision, which normally indicates a good game, but I have a few issues with it.

1. I am always frustrated that my attempts to look ahead never work out.  "Pick a strategy and stick with it" philosophy will kill you.
2. The game ends a little too early... momentum hasn't had time to settle with any player.
3. It's too easy to develop tunnel vision.  You have to watch every aspect of the game.  Once players figure this out, every turns takes an eternity.
4. A player who is perceived as winning has zero chance.  Mob mentality rules.

All my friends love this game, but after many plays I still haven't warmed up to it.

RATING: 8
There's something very challenging and addictive about this game.  Certainly, the pastel camels are appealing, but that's not enough to keep me interested in a game, just to get me to notice it.  The territorial aspect of the game initially didn't appeal to me, nor did the glacial pace of the progress (two camels at a time, usually placed on different trains, makes for a long development time, even though the turns are short), but the challenge of walling off certain areas of the board while also going for the oases makes the game very interesting.  It's very easy to play one game after another, and it challenges you to make some tough decisions each turn.

4 plays: 3 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
From the award-winning game designer Reiner Knizia comes a game of strategy, patience, and cool plastic camels! The desert is still treacherous, mysterious, and without mercy. But for those willing to risk the dangers of the shifting, sun-baked sands, the desert holds riches beyond compare.
\13\\13\
In Through the Desert, two to five players each control a tribe of nomads vying for control of the desert. By establishing caravans and taking over oases, the players gain points as their tribes increase in power.
Strategy is essential in deciding how and where to build your tribe's caravans. There are multiple ways to gain points and several ways to win. Should you try to build the longest caravan? Or should you dominate the desert's oases? Don't forget to keep an eye on your opponents' caravans, or you may find your own tribe cut off from valuable water holes.

RATING: 8
Extremely tense game and very meaty; it packs more tension into a one hour game than most "epic" games I've played. I like the multiple paths to victory that the various ways to score offer, and how highly interactive it is. There are two minor, related issues that keep me from rating it more highly: it's extremely difficult to recover from poor initial placement, and this therefore gives this a much worse kingmaker problem than many games if you're playing with more than 2. On the other hand, you could say the same thing about Settlers, and this plays in half the time, so it's not nearly as frustrating.

RATING: 5
Abstracts are not my favorite. Through the Desert is very clearly an abstract, however, it was somewhat interesting to play and pretty snappy after I was taught the rules. The teacher of this game said that detractors of this game always point to the necessity of a good starting position and how the rest of the game really just plays out from there. I’ve only played once, but I think I see where they’re coming from.

I’d play this again, but I’d prefer playing most other games.

RATING: 6
While a lot of people feel this is a Go variant... I think the decisions feel a lot more like playing hex... so its a variant of a go variant I guess :D

The starting positions are pretty important, but once you get past that bit of learning curve its easy to set yourself up for success.  I like gathering point chips, I like connecting oasises, I like competing for longest camel train and getting in my opponents way.  But as I've played more Knizia games with interesting scoring twists and deeper decisions despite equally simple rules this one has gotten less and less play and really isn't something I would reach for over a handful of other similar games in my collection (Taluva, Samurai, Pompeii, Ingenious, Hey That's my Fish!). Bummer to fall out of love with a game but I'd still play someone else's copy if offered.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Lots of tough choices in Through the Desert.  Having 5 caravans to deal with almost seem superfluous at first as you really have only enough turns to extend 2 or at most 3 of them fully, but I suppose it does give you more options to work with once other people start to cut your's off at various point in the game.  It definitely seems overwhelming in the beginning, having so many things you could do but only a limited actions to perform.  The flow of the game becomes more chaotic as you increase the number of participants, so I imagine with 2-player it is much more strategic and require careful planning, much like in chess and go.  Timing is everything.
